Title: Detection and Mode-Identification of Multiple Change Points in Tensor Factor Models

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.11300v1

Published Time: Tue, 14 Apr 2026 02:05:51 GMT

Number of Pages: 165

Markdown Content:
# Detection and Mode-Identification of Multiple Change Points in Tensor Factor Models 

Yuqi Zhang вҲ—1, Zetai Cen вҖ 1, and Haeran Cho вҖЎ11School of Mathematics, University of Bristol 

Abstract 

We study the problems arising from modeling high-dimensional tensor-valued time series under a Tucker decomposition-based factor model with multiple structural change points. First, we propose an algorithm for detecting the multiple change points, which utilizes the low-rank structure of the data for statistical and computational efficiency. Also, the multi-dimensional array setting poses unique challenges, as some changes are associated with a subset of the modes, and the changes in different modes may interact with one another. Recognizing these, we investigate the problem of identifying each change with the tensor modes post-segmentation. To this end, we formalize the mode-identifiability of each change and propose an algorithm for detecting the modes at which the data are undergoing a mode-identifiable shift. We establish the consistency of both change point detection and mode-identification methods under a weak moment condition, and demonstrate their good performance on simu-lated datasets where, in particular, it is shown that the mode-identification step can improve the post-segmentation estimation of the mode-wise loading space. Additionally we analyze the datasets on New York City taxi usage and FamaвҖ“French portfolio returns using the proposed suite of methods. 

Key words and phrases: Change point detection, mode-identifiability, tensor factor model, Tucker decomposition.

41 Introduction 

High-dimensional time series are increasingly observed in the form of tensors, where each obser-vation is a multi-dimensional array organized along several modes. Examples include dynamic transport and trade networks across product groups, multi-dimensional finance panels indexed by firms, characteristics and time, spatiotemporal environmental fields, and biomedical imaging data, to name a few (Li et al., 2020; Chen et al., 2022; Sedighin, 2024; Babii et al., 2025). Such tensor formatting not only preserves the innate interpretation of the data, but also can underline inherent structure therein. Tensor factor models provide a natural framework for analyzing such data by imposing a low-rank representation. Specifically, each tensor-valued observation is assumed to be driven by a handful of latent factors, as characterized by some mode-specific loading matrices, thereby achieving dimension reduction while preserving the role of each mode (Wang et al., 2019; Chen et al., 2022; Han et al., 2024b). Based on the two popular tensor decompositions (Kolda and Bader, 2009), Tucker and CanDecomp/PARAFAC (CP) factor models have frequently been studied, where the latter is a special case of the former, and the recent literature has seen a wide range of developments from determining the ranks (Han et al., 2022), to estimating the mode-wise loading spaces and latent factors (Chen and Lam, 2024; Han et al., 2024a; Barigozzi et al., 2026, 2025a) and handling missing observations by imputation (Cen and Lam, 2025b). While most of these contributions are made assuming that the factor structure is time-invariant, departure from such an assumption is often observed in many applications. Macroeconomic and financial dependence patterns may shift after major policy changes, crises or market re-organizations, leading to structural changes in how certain factors drive the observed time series (Stock and Watson, 2009; Breitung and Eickmeier, 2011; Chen et al., 2014). In these scenarios, ignoring changes could undermine parameter estimation and mask the interpretation of how the latent structure evolves. The problem of change point detection has studied extensively in vector time series factor mod-eling. Existing work includes tests for structural instability in factor loadings (Chen et al., 2014; Yamamoto and Tanaka, 2015; Baltagi et al., 2017), estimation of a single break date (Baltagi et al., 2017; Duan et al., 2023, 2025), inference for break dates (Bai et al., 2024), and multiple change point detection algorithms (Chan et al., 2014; Barigozzi et al., 2018; Baltagi et al., 2021; Li et al., 2023; Bai et al., 2024; Barigozzi et al., 2025b). By contrast, the problem of detecting change points in matrix or, more generally, tensor time series remains largely unexplored. For matrix factor models with potential changes, He et al. (2024) proposed an online monitoring 5scheme, while Peng et al. (2025a,b) developed offline procedures. Beyond the matrix settings, Huang et al. (2022); PeЛҮ sta et al. (2025) study the problem of detecting changes in the mean of a tensor-variate sequence, Zhang et al. (2024); Wang et al. (2025) investigate the problem in dynamic networks, and Anastasiou and Cribben (2025) propose a practical approach to detect changes in cryptocurrency networks by leveraging CP decomposition. Despite the growing interests in tensor data with change points, a formal treatment towards offline detection in tensor factor models is still absent, to the best of our knowledge. Moreover, due to the multi-dimensional array nature of the data, changes in a tensor factor model may affect one or several modes of the tensor data and manifest differently across modes, and these changes in different modes may interact with one another. Vectorizing the data would annihilate this property, thus obscuring the relations between any detected change and the tensor modes, which calls for methods that preserve and exploit the tensor structure. Recognizing these new challenges, we develop a suite of methods for modeling tensor time series under a factor model with multiple changes. Firstly, we propose an algorithm for detecting mul-tiple change points in the tensor factor model. It extends the idea often utilized in the vector factor modeling literature, that different types of changes in the factor structure are observation-ally equivalent to the changes in the covariance of so-called pseudo-factor of a fixed dimension (Han and Inoue, 2015; Baltagi et al., 2021; Duan et al., 2023), to the tensor setting. This is particularly beneficial for the tensor-valued data that quickly becomes high-dimensional: For a series of order-K tensors Xt вҲҲ Rp1Г—В·В·В·Г— pK , our proposed method performs multiple change point detection on the objects of dimension PKk=1 rk(rk + 1) /2, where rkвҖҷs denote the dimensions of the order-K pseudo-factor tensor which are typically much smaller than pk.We also formulate and study the problem of mode-identification for tensor factor models with multiple change points, a first contribution of its kind. We formally characterize the mode-identifiability of a change, which enables the selection of the tensor modes that undergo identifiable shifts at each change point once the change points are detected. A direct downstream benefit of mode-identification is that it enables mode-informed loading space estimation which brings demonstrable numerical benefits over estimating the loading spaces segment by segment. Under a weak moment condition, we establish the consistency of the combined methodology, both in estimating the total number and locations of the change points and in selecting the subset of modes undergoing identifiable changes at each change point. The rest of this paper is organized as follows. Section 2 introduces the tensor factor model with multiple change points and defines the notion of mode-identifiability of a change. In Section 3, we describe the methods for multiple change point detection and post-detection mode-identification, 6with a detailed discussion on the selection of tuning parameters. Section 4 presents the assump-tions and establishes the asymptotic consistency of the proposed methods. Section 5 reports the simulation results and in Section 6, we analyze the datasets on New York City taxi usage and FamaвҖ“French portfolio returns. Section 7 concludes the paper, and all the proofs, additional discussions and simulation results are deferred to the supplement.

2.1 Tensor factor model with multiple change points 

Consider a mean-zero time series of order-K tensors Xt вҲҲ Rp1Г—В·В·В·Г— pK , t вҲҲ [T ], with K вүҘ 1, which follows a Tucker tensor factor model with q change points such that

Here, Ft вҲҲ Rr1Г—В·В·В·Г— rK denotes the core factor tensor with its dimensions rkвҖҷs fixed regardless of 7growing pk, Ct denotes the common component driven by the core factor, and Et is the idiosyncratic component unaccounted by Ft. We denote by Оӣ j,k вҲҲ Rpk Г—rk the mode-k factor loading matrix in the j-th segment such that at each change point Оёj , at least one of the loading matrices undergoes a shift. We permit the numbers of factors to change such that the column rank of each Оӣ j,k , which effectively represents the mode-k factor number in the j-th segment, may be strictly smaller than 

rk; later we introduce an alternative representation of the model (2.1) with which the meaning of 

rk is made clearer. Throughout, we make the following condition on {F t}Tt=1 :

Condition 1. At any t вҲҲ [T ], the elements of the core factor Ft are uncorrelated with each other and have zero mean and unit variance, i.e. Cov(vec( Ft)) = Ir.

For each given j, while the space spanned by the columns of each Оӣ j,k , i.e. col(Оӣ j,k ), is uniquely defined, the loading matrices and the core factor tensor are not identifiable regardless of K. From this perspective, Condition 1 is akin to the assumption commonly found in the literature on vector factor modeling (Bai, 2003), if Ft admits a separable covariance structure (Hoff, 2011), i.e. Cov(vec( Ft)) = вҠ—1

2.2 Mode-identifiability at each change point 

Inspecting (2.3) reveals that the size of change Пү(k) 

> j

in Definition 1, depends not only on the change in the mode-k transformation matrix, but also those corresponding to other modes. Accordingly, the tensor setting with K > 1 brings complications previously unobserved in the vector factor modeling literature, and naturally raises the following questions. (Q1) Can we identify all the modes that undergo a shift at the j-th change point, i.e. all k вҲҲ [K]such that Aj,k Мё = Aj+1 ,k ?(Q2) If multiple modes have Aj,k Мё = Aj+1 ,k at the j-th change point, can these changes cancel each other out, leading to Пүj = 0? 9Although the problem of change point detection under matrix factor models has previously been studied, these questions have not been addressed: He et al. (2024) focus on the situation where a change occurs in a single mode only, in developing and analyzing an online change test; Peng et al. (2025a,b) propose to detect changes in the covariance of vec( Gt) and thus ignore the cross-mode interactions. We make a first contribution in addressing the inherently unique challenges brought by the tensor setting. To this end, we first define the notion of mode-identifiability of a change. 

Definition 2 (Mode-identification of changes) . Consider the model in (2.2) and suppose that Condition 1 holds. Then at Оёj for some j вҲҲ [q], the mode-k change from Aj,k to Aj+1 ,k is defined to be mode-unidentifiable if and only if there exists a set of matrices {Bв„“}в„“вҲҲ[K]\{ k} such that 

Ft Г—в„“вҲҲ[K]\{ k} Aj+1 ,в„“ Г—k Aj+1 ,k = Ft Г—в„“вҲҲ[K]\{ k} Bв„“ Г—k Aj,k .

Otherwise the change point Оёj is mode-k identifiable. A change point is referred to as mode-unidentifiable when it is mode-k unidentifiable for all k вҲҲ [K]; otherwise it is mode-identifiable. 

With Definition 2, we can associate each change point Оёj with the subset of tensor modes k вҲҲ [K]for which it is mode-identifiable, such that characterizing the mode-k change only depends upon the relation between Aj,k and Aj+1 ,k regardless of the transformation matrices at modes в„“Мё = k.We justify this notion of mode-(un)identifiability with the following theorem. 

Theorem 1 (The only type of mode-unidentifiable change) . Consider the model in (2.2) and suppose that Condition 1 holds. Then for any j вҲҲ [q] and k вҲҲ [K], the mode-k change is mode-unidentifiable for Оёj if and only if Aj+1 ,k = cA j,k Q for some constant c and orthogonal matrix Q.

Notice that under Condition 1, the transformation matrices Aj are identifiable up to orthogonal transforms (cf. Lemma B.2). Therefore, Theorem 1 establishes that the only type of mode-unidentifiable change is in the form of a scalar multiplication. Jointly, Definition 2 and Theorem 1 allow us to regard the changes across different modes individually in higher-order tensor factor models. Back to the earlier questions, Theorem 1 directly addresses (Q1). As for (Q2), combined with Lemma B.1 in the supplement, the theorem shows that any mode-identifiable change is detectable (in the sense that Пүj > 0) and, in fact, the cross-mode cancellation of the change occurs in the trivial scenario only: When Оёj is mode-unidentifiable with the scalar multiples on all modes canceling one another. For completeness, we further categorize the types of mode-identifiable changes in Section A.2. In summary, the model (2.1) poses an interesting challenge of mode-identification which, following the detection of multiple change points, can enrich their interpretation and reveal cross-mode 10 interactions. We propose a mode-identification algorithm in Section 3.2 that accounts for the stochasticity arising from the change point detection step. 

## 3 Methodology 

3.1 Change point detection 

Recall that under the model (2.2), we are able to detect the change points by scanning for the changes in the covariance of the global pseudo-factor tensor Gt. As a first step for estimating the unobservable Gt, we estimate the mode-k loading matrix Оӣ k from the (scaled) mode-k sample covariance matrix of Xt for each k вҲҲ [K], i.e. bО“(k)

Specifically, we embed this statistic in a multiscale search based on seeded intervals (KovВҙ acs et al., 2023) and the narrowest-over-threshold principle (Baranowski et al., 2019), the combination of which has successfully been applied to a wide range of change point detection problems. Specifically, we evaluate Ta,П„,b over a collection of the seeded intervals generated as 

M = 

> вҢҠОјTвҢӢ

> h=1

: i вҲҲ вҢҲT /m hвҢү вҲ’ 1, m h = T / 2ho

, (3.4) with ОјT вүҚ log 2 log( T ). By construction, the number of intervals in M is O(log( T )), and the lengths of the intervals are at least of order T / log( T ). In the literature on change point de-tection under factor models, it is often assumed that the change points are linearly spaced (see Assumption 3), which ensures that pseudo-factors induced by the changes are well-captured by the global estimation procedure leading to bGt, t вҲҲ [T ]. This allows us, with the thus-constructed 

M, to systematically zoom in the neighborhoods of individual change points and isolate each in a 12 sufficiently large seeded interval for its detection and estimation. Next, we iteratively select the shortest interval, say ( a, b ] вҲҲ M, over which the maximal local covariance difference aggregated over the K modes, as measured by max a+П–T <П„ <b вҲ’П–T Ta,П„,b , ex-ceeds a given threshold ПҖT,p ; if there are ties, we select the interval with the largest detector statistic. The trimming parameter П–T , as well as the choice of the finest level ОјT for the seeded interval construction, are introduced to effectively control for the errors arising from scanning the data multiple times, which becomes more challenging under the weaker moment condition we impose on the data (see Assumptions 1 and 4 below). This step returns a seeded interval containing exactly one change point with high probability, effectively breaking down the multiple change point problem to that of detecting each change point individually. Then we identify a change point estimator as bОё = arg max a+П–T <П„ <b вҲ’П–T Ta,П„,b , remove any intervals in M containing 

bОё from future consideration, and repeatedly perform the above steps until no seeded interval is left to be considered. Referred to as tensor factor model segmentation algorithm (TFMseg), its full description is given in Algorithm 1.

Remark 1 (Utilizing mode-wise separability for change point detection.) . In the context of ma-trix factor time series segmentation, Peng et al. (2025a,b) propose to search for change points in 

13 the r Г— r covariance of vec( bGt) where r = Q3 

> k=1

rk. While not reported here, in preliminary nu-merical experiments, we considered its tensor-analogue by modifying the CUSUM statistic Ma,П„,b 

accordingly while keeping the rest of the algorithm identical, and found that the resultant proce-dure suffered from the relatively large dimensionality. For example, for the order-3 tensor factor time series in Section 5.1 with (r1, r 2, r 3) = (3 , 3, 3) , this approach handles a CUSUM process of dimension 27 Г— 28 /2 = 378 , compared to 3 Г— 3 Г— 4/2 = 18 in the case of TFMseg. This illustrates the advantage of the proposed detector statistic which utilizes the separability of the covariance of 

Gt implied by Condition 1, in further reducing the dimensionality of the structure of interest from 

r(r + 1) /2 to PKk=1 rk(rk + 1) /2, the gap between which grows with the tensor order K.

Remark 2 (Change point detection with missing data) . Often the data contain missingness particularly in high-dimensional time series. Analyzing a fully-observed subset of the data only, may significantly reduce the realized sample size and potentially compromise the interpretation of the outcome, all of which may be intensified for tensor data. Common techniques to circumvent missing observations can be categorized as likelihood-based methods (Little and An, 2004; Garcia et al., 2010), inverse probability weighting (Johnson et al., 2008; Seaman and White, 2013), and imputation (Kazijevs and Samad, 2023; Deng and Lumley, 2024), and the problem of handling missingness in the change point detection framework has attracted some attention (Xie et al., 2012; Follain et al., 2022; Liu and Safikhani, 2025). We may leverage an imputation procedure proposed by Cen and Lam (2025b) for tensor time series admitting a representation in (2.1) 

(without change points). Specifically, noting that the detector statistic in (3.3) only requires some estimator of the core factors, we may employ the estimated factor series (which contains no missing data) in Equation (3.7) of Cen and Lam (2025b) to plug in (3.2) , with the rest of the steps of TFMseg remaining the same. We empirically validate this modification of TFMseg in Section 6.2 and Appendix D.5.

Remark 3 (Mode-informed estimation of loading spaces) . Once the change points are detected as in Section 3.1, we may estimate the mode-k loading space for the j-th segment, span(Оӣ j,k ),using the section of the data over the interval (bОёjвҲ’1, bОёj ]. However, span(Оӣ j,k ) for some mode-k

does not necessarily vary at every change point, either as Aj,k = Aj+1 ,k , or as the change is mode-

k unidentifiable (Theorem 1). Taking advantage of the proposed mode-identification method, it is possible to utilize the maximally selected data section for mode-wise loading space estimation. Specifically, for each mode k вҲҲ [K], we concatenate all the intervals where no change is deemed mode-k identifiable within, say (bОёj1 , bОёj2 ] for some 0 вүӨ j1 < j 2 вүӨ bq + 1 , and estimate the load-

15 ing space therein. Upon consistently estimating Kj , this mode-informed approach is expected to improve upon the naive, segment-wise estimator, which we illustrate empirically in Section 5.4.

Weight matrix WП„ . The asymptotic consistency in change point detection is established for any positive definite matrix W with bounded spectrum, as WП„ (see Theorem 2). In the context of detecting mean shifts in multivariate time series, Cho et al. (2026) discuss the computational, numerical and statistical trade-off between the use of a weight matrix estimating the full (long-run) covariance of the noise, and that only contains its diagonal elements. They show that none of the two choices uniformly outperforms the other in terms of the detection power, and the latter choice is to be preferred for its numerical stability for moderately large dimensions, particularly as the inverse of the matrix is used in the detector statistic. From these considerations, we choose to use as W the matrix containing the diagonal entries of the following Bartlett kernel long-run covariance matrix estimator cW0, an approach also taken by Barigozzi et al. (2025b): With

Seeded intervals and trimming parameter. We set ОјT in (3.4) so that the minimum interval length is 0 .5T / log( T ), i.e. ОјT = log 2(4 log( T )). Accordingly, we set П–T = 0 .25 T / log( T ). 

Threshold ПҖT,p for change point detection. Theorem 2 below gives a rate range for ПҖT,p 

which, however, involves quantities that are unknown in practice. This is a common issue in change point detection where a practical solution is through large-scale simulations, an approach which we also adopt here. Specifically, we simulate tensor time series under the null model (q = 0, see Appendix D.1). For each realization, we record max (s,e ]вҲҲM max s<П„ <e Ts,П„,e , take the empirical 0 .9-quantiles of these maxima over 100 realizations for each combination of dimensions 16 and other data generating parameters, and regress them on the functions of T and ( r1, r 2, r 3). We use the fitted model ( R2adj is roughly 0.94) with T and brk as regressors, to determine ПҖT,p ; see Appendix C.1 for the complete description.

pвҲ’1); see Appendix C.2 for details of the experiment. 

## 4 Theory 

4.1 Assumptions 

We introduce the assumptions on the degree of serial and cross-sectional dependence in {X t}Tt=1 ,and the strength of the factors under the model (2.2). We first define a class of general linear tensor time series. 

Definition 3. A tensor time series {Y t}tвҲҲZ with Yt вҲҲ Rp1Г—... Г—pK , is a (ОҪ, ОІ )-general linear tensor time series for some ОҪ вүҘ 4 and ОІ вүҘ 0, if and only if it holds that Yt = P 

> hвүҘ0

ay,h Xy,t вҲ’h where (i) the innovation process {X y,t } has independent elements with zero mean, unit variance, and uni-formly bounded ОҪ-th order moments, i.e. denoting the elements of Xy,t by Xy,t, i, i = ( i1, . . . , i K ),there exists some constant CОҪ > 0 depending on ОҪ, such that sup tвҲҲZ max iвҲҲQKk=1 [pk ] E(|X y,t, i|ОҪ ) вүӨ

CОҪ ; (ii) the scalar coefficients ay,h satisfy P

> s=m

|ay,s | вүӨ cОІ .

Definition 3 formalizes the data generating process first used in Chen and Lam (2024), which is convenient in modeling tensor time series with temporal dependence structure. Essentially, for each i вҲҲ QKk=1 [pk], the time series {Y t, i}tвҲҲZ follows a general linear process with weak serial correlation, bounded ОҪ-th moment, and ОІ characterizing the tail summability. By construction, the linearity of Yt in {X y,t вҲ’h}hвүҘ0, is preserved under various operations on Yt such as vectorization, unfolding and tensor reshaping. In the definition, (iii) is implied by (ii) e.g. when ОІ = 0. With Definition 3, and вҲҶ j = min( Оёj вҲ’ОёjвҲ’1, Оё j+1 вҲ’Оёj ) denoting the spacing between Оёj and its neighboring change points for each j вҲҲ [q], we make the following assumptions. 

Assumption 1 (Core factor) . The series {F t}tвҲҲZ is a (ОҪ, ОІ )-general linear tensor time series. 

17 Assumption 2 (Global factor loadings) . For each k вҲҲ [K], there exists some constant cО» such that (i) the largest elements of Оӣk is bounded by cО» in modulus and (ii) pвҲ’1

Assumption 4 (Idiosyncratic noise) . There are (ОҪ, ОІ )-general linear tensor time series {F e,t }tвҲҲZ

and {Пөt}tвҲҲZ independent of each other, such that the noise Et, t вҲҲ [T ], admits the representation 

Et = Fe,t Г—Kk=1 Ae,k + ОЈ Пө в—Ұ Пөt, where each Ae,k вҲҲ Rpk Г—re,k satisfies вҲҘAe,k вҲҘ1 = O(1) and вҲҘAe,k вҲҘвҲһ =

O(1) , and the order-K tensor ОЈПө has uniformly bounded elements. Finally, {F e,t }tвҲҲZ and {Пөt}tвҲҲZ

are independent of {F t}tвҲҲZ.

Under Assumption 1, we have Ft satisfy Condition 1. Assumption 2 is a tensor analogue of the condition found in Bai and Ng (2013), and similar conditions are made in the literature on factor modeling for vector- (Stock and Watson, 2002), matrix- (Yu et al., 2022), and tensor-valued (Barigozzi et al., 2026) time series. Assumption 3 regularizes the transformation matrices so that, together with Assumption 2, the global loading matrices Оӣ k, k вҲҲ [K], can be estimated consistently. Part (i) prevents the factor structure from vanishing along some modes in any segment; parts (ii) and (iii), in combination with Assumption 2, generalize the standard full column rank condition imposed on the loading matrices in factor models, to the change point setting. In the change point literature, when K = 1, our part (ii) is akin to Assumption 5 (iii) in Barigozzi et al. (2025b) which constrains the covariance of the pseudo-factor rather than the transformation matrices; part (iii) boils down to Assumption 11 (i) in Duan et al. (2023), see also their Remark 2 for more discussion. The linear spacing condition made in (iv) is invariably found in the related literature; see, for example, Barigozzi et al. (2018, 2025b); Baltagi et al. (2021); Duan et al. (2025). We may relax this which, however, calls for the expansion of the interval collection M with ОјT вүҚ log 2(T ) (see (3.4)), and has further implication on the detection and estimation performance, see Appendix A.3 for detail. 18 Under Assumption 4, the idiosyncratic component is decomposed into a factor-driven component with approximately sparse loadings, and a linear tensor time series, and thus contains both cross-sectional and serial correlation. At the same time, the cross-sectional correlations are sufficiently weak so that with Assumptions 2 and 3, there exists a large gap between the rk-th and the ( rk +1)-th largest eigenvalues of the mode-k covariance of mat k(Xt) for all k вҲҲ [K], which ensures the asymptotic identifiability of the (unobserved) Ct and Et across all the modes. We specify our requirements on ( ОҪ, ОІ ) in Assumptions 1 and 4 later; it suffices to assume that ОҪ вүҘ 4 and ОІ вүҘ 0for consistent estimation of Оӣ k (Proposition 1), whereas we require that ОҪ > 8 and ОІ > 1 for the consistency in change point detection and mode-identification (Theorems 2 and 3). 

Assumption 5 (Signal-to-noise ratio) . The jump magnitudes Пүj , j вҲҲ [q], defined in Definition 1 satisfy min jвҲҲ[q]

pвҲҶj Пүj /plog( T ) вҶ’ вҲһ and p min jвҲҲ[q] Пүj вҶ’ вҲһ as min( T, p ) вҶ’ вҲһ .

Assumption 6 (Dimensionality) . pвҲ’1вҲҡT = O(plog( T )) as min( T, p 1, . . . , p K ) вҶ’ вҲһ .

Assumption 5 permits Пүj вҶ’ 0 as min( T, p 1, . . . , p K ) вҶ’ вҲһ , at a sufficiently slow rate and accord-ingly, the rate of estimation for each Оёj explicitly depends on Пүj (Theorem 2). Assumption 6 is weaker than the conditions on the relative divergence of p and T found in Baltagi et al. (2021); Duan et al. (2023); Bai et al. (2024); Barigozzi et al. (2025b) for change point detection and testing, that pвҲ’1вҲҡT = o(1). Under Assumptions 3 (iv) and 6, the second requirement in Assumption 5 implies the first. Theorem 2 establishes that TFMseg consistently estimates both the total number and locations of the change points, which holds for any positive definite matrix W with bounded spectra in place of WП„ in (3.3); the specific form of W may only affect the finite-sample power. This viewpoint is in line with the multivariate change point detection framework of Kirch et al. (2015), where the weighting matrix in their CUSUM statistic is treated as a tuning parameter, and structured choices are discussed as a way of balancing between numerical stability and detection power. The choice of ПҖT,p is compatible with Assumption 5. The rate of estimation for each Оёj is characterized by the change size Пүj , in the sense that |bОёj вҲ’ Оёj | = OP (ПүвҲ’2 

> j

П…T ), i.e. larger changes are estimated with better accuracy. With П…T вҶ’ вҲһ arbitrarily slowly, under Assumption 5, we have |bОёj вҲ’Оёj |/вҲҶj = oP (1), i.e. the change point estimators are consistent in the re-scaled time. We remark that Theorem 2 is established under a condition on the tail behavior of {F t}tвҲҲZ and {E t}tвҲҲZ

characterized through ОҪ > 8, which is comparable to those found in vector (Duan et al., 2023; Bai et al., 2024) and matrix (Peng et al., 2025a,b) settings. We may relax this and require ОҪ > 4, which calls for e.g. a stronger condition on the signal-to-noise, namely T вҲ’ОҪ/ 8+1 = o(min jвҲҲ[q] Пүj

pвҲҶj ), than that found in Assumption 5. Finally, we establish the consistency in mode-identification at each change point, a first result in its kind, which builds upon the consistency in change point detection in Theorem 2.

In the main text, we report the results on change point detection and mode-identification under the above model with varying 

T вҲҲ { 400 , 800 , 1600 , 3200 } and ( p1, p 2, p 3) вҲҲ { (10 , 10 , 10) , (10 , 10 , 100) , (10 , 20 , 40) , (20 , 20 , 20) },when the change points are вҖңunbalancedвҖқ, i.e. Оҳ = вҢҠ0.25 T вҢӢ, вҢҠ0.5T вҢӢ, вҢҠ0.625 T вҢӢ , and the data are serially correlated with ПҒf = 0 .7, which we consider as the more challenging scenario. For each setting, N = 100 realizations are generated. Additionally, we consider further change point scenarios as well as the null scenarios (i.e. Оҳ = вҲ…), and explore the performance of the proposed methods for change point detection and mode-identification in Appendices D.2 and D.3, respectively; we verify the discussion in Remark 3 on im-proving the mode-wise loading space estimation via mode-identification, see Appendix D.4; finally, we report in Appendix D.5 the performance TFMseg in the presence of missingness with the mod-ification as discussed in Remark 2. Throughout, we apply TFMseg and the mode-identification procedure with the tuning parameters generated as in Section 3.3. 21 5.2 Results for change point detection 

For each n вҲҲ [N ], denote by bq(n) and bОё(n) 

> j

the number of change point estimators and their locations for the n-th realization. Figure 1 displays the distributions of the change point estimators 

bОё(n) 

> j

, j вҲҲ [bq(n)], returned by TFMseg over N = 100 realizations, across varying dimensions and sample size. We additionally report the accuracy of the change point estimators conditional on the successful detection of all three change points, by considering the realizations where bq(n) = 3 and bОё(n) 

> j

вҲҲ (( ОёjвҲ’1 +Оёj )/2, (Оёj +Оёj+1 )/2] for all j вҲҲ [3]; the index set of those realizations is denoted by bD вҠӮ [N ]. For comparison, we consider the binary segmentation procedure of Bai et al. (2024) which, referred to as вҖңLRвҖқ, recursively performs likelihood-ratio tests. As the method is proposed for vector time series, we first vectorize the tensor time series prior to its application. In its implementation, we set the minimum segment length to be 0 .5T / log( T ) while all other tuning parameters are set as recommended by the authors. Overall, the performance of both methods improves as T and ( p1, p 2, p 3) grow, which is in line with Assumption 5. TFMseg tends to perform better than LR, particularly when T and/or (p1, p 2, p 3) are smaller, and the difference in performance is more striking when focusing on how frequently all three change points are detected by each method. This is not too surprising as the vectorization step taken by LR ignores the tensor-structure of the data, see also Remark 1. The tensor-based approach is also beneficial computationally, and TFMseg is considerably faster than LR as the dimensions increase (see Figure D.3). Additionally, the results in Appendix D.2 indicate that TFMsegвҖҷs performance improves in the absence of serial correlations (with ПҒf = 0), and its calibration suggested in Section 3.3 works well so that TFMseg returns few spurious estimator in the absence of any change point (see Appendix D.2.1).

Table 1 shows that the proposed mode-identification method performs well, with TPR and FPR improving as the sample size and the dimensions increase. Additional numerical experiments not reported here, indicate that the relatively lower TPR when T = 400, is attributed to that

Appendix D.3 reports the results from additional scenarios with more complex change structures including mode-k unidentifiable changes for some modes. 24 5.4 Results for mode-wise loading space estimation 

We examine the impact of mode-identification for the downstream task of estimating the loading spaces. To this end, we compare the вҖңmode-informedвҖқ approach discussed in Remark 3, against the naive approach that estimates the column space col(Оӣ j,k ) over each estimated segment for each k вҲҲ [K], focusing on the single change point scenario ( q = 1) where K = 3, ( r1, r 2, r 3) = (3 , 3, 3), Оё1 = вҢҠT / 2вҢӢ and K1 = {1}; we refer to Appendix D.4 for the complete description of the experiments. After applying TFMseg and the mode-identification procedure to N = 100 realizations per setting, we denote by eD вҠӮ bD вҠӮ [N ], the index set of realizations where the single change is detected and bK1 = K1 is returned. On those realizations, noting that col(Оӣ 1,k ) = col(Оӣ 2,k ) = col(Оӣ k) for k вҲҲ { 2, 3}, the mode-informed approach estimates such col(Оӣ k) using the whole sample, while col(Оӣ j, 1) is estimated separately on ( bОёjвҲ’1, bОёj ] for each j вҲҲ [2]. We report the loading space estimation error as measured by (D.1) in Appendix D.4, see Figure 2 for a representative example. Overall, the mode-k loading estimation error decreases with p-k, in line with Proposition 1. Also, it is apparent that the mode-informed approach benefits from pooling the information across the entire data for the modes k / вҲҲ K 1.(10,10,10) (10,10,100) (10,100,10) (100,10,10)

Figure 2: Boxplots of mode-wise loading estimation errors with T = 400 and varying ( p1, p 2, p 3)(left to right). Segment pre (resp. Segment post) refers to segment-wise estimation errors using the data before (resp. after) the change point estimator bОё1.25 6 Real data applications 

6.1 New York City Yellow Taxi data 

We analyze the New York City (NYC) Yellow Taxi trip records released by the cityвҖҷs Taxi and Limousine Commission (TLC). 1 We focus on trips within Manhattan, which account for the majority of the observations. The pick-up and drop-off locations are aggregated into p1 = p2 = 69 pre-defined taxi zones, and each day is partitioned into p3 = 24 hourly intervals. Then on each business day t, we observe an order-3 tensor Xt вҲҲ R69 Г—69 Г—24 , where the entry xi1,i 2,i 3,t records the number of trips from zone i1 to zone i2 during the i3-th hourly interval, over the period from 1 January 2013 to 31 December 2022 ( T = 2519). Inspired by the discussion on the same dataset in Chen et al. (2022), we set the pseudo-factor dimensions to ( r1, r 2, r 3) = (4 , 2, 2) for change point detection and mode-identification; all other tuning parameters set as in Section 3.3. As comparison, we apply the recursive likelihood-ratio testing procedure of Bai et al. (2024) (вҖңLRвҖқ) to the vectorized time series similarly as in Section 5.2. TFMseg detects four change points (2013-10-16, 2014-10-15, 2016-10-19, 2020-03-06) while LR detects three (2016-03-21, 2020-03-10, 2021-04-02). The two methods agree most closely on the disruption in March 2020, with estimated break dates only a few business days apart. Outside this episode, however, the estimated change locations differ substantially, as visualized in Figure 3. The breaks in March 2020 has a clear interpretation: NYC confirmed its first COVID-19 case on 1 March 2020, and TLC reported that the transportation demand fell to 84% of the pre-COVID level by the beginning of April, and both TFMseg and LR detect this pandemic-related change at a plausible date. On the other hand, the changes detected prior to 2017 may be related to the market shrinkage of traditional yellow taxi service, due to the rapidly growing app-based for-hire services. For example, TLC proceedings in October 2014 indicated that the expansion of Uber had become major regulatory concerns. The mode-identification results shown in Figure 4 are consistent with these findings. The first two change point estimators returned by TFMseg are not assigned to any mode, reflecting a change in the overall market size. The estimator on 2016-10-19 is associated with the pick-up and drop-off locations and appear location-related. Interestingly, the 2020-03-06 change is associated with all three modes, reflecting again the overall change to NYC traffic pattern due to COVID-19.

j вҲҘ > О¶ T,p .27 6.2 FamaвҖ“French portfolio returns 

We study the FamaвҖ“French portfolio returns data, 2 where the stocks are sorted into ten groups by market equity (ME) and ten groups by book-to-equity ratio (BE) to form 10 Г— 10 portfolios at each time stamp. We consider the time series of order-2 tensors Xt вҲҲ R10 Г—10 on value- and equal-weighted returns, respectively, where the former weights stocks by market capitalization and the latter assigns equal weights within each portfolio. We use the monthly data from January 1974 to June 2021 ( T = 570). The data are not fully observed with 0.28% missingness, and complete observations are available from July 2009 only. We leverage the approach described in Remark 2 and estimate the pseudo-factors directly from the incomplete data with ( br1, br2) = (2 , 2) (Cen and Lam, 2025b). With all the tuning parameters set as in Section 3.3, TFMseg detects a single change occurring at 1998-11-01 in the value-weighted series and at 2002-06-01 in the equal-weighted series. These dates lie close to the peak of the dot-com boom; Appendix E presents figures that illustrate the overall market-wide variation along with the estimated change point. When applied to the vectorized time series, LR (Bai et al., 2024) does not return any change, even when the tuning parameter controlling the minimum length of the segment, or the factor numbers are varied. The mode-identification method does not assign this change to either mode for both series, as all scaled mode-identification statistics, вҲҘbОһ(k)1 вҲҘ/(T вҲ’1/2 + pвҲ’1), fall below 3 .5 (cf. О¶T,p = 3 .5( T вҲ’1/2 +

pвҲ’1)). For the equal-weighted series, however, the scaled statistics are closer to the benchmark value 3 .5, with values 2 .53 for ME mode and 3 .09 for BE mode, whereas the corresponding values for the value-weighted series are markedly smaller at 0 .95 and 1 .77 each. This suggests that, although the detected changes for both series are mode-unidentifiable, the change in the equal-weighted series exhibits a relatively stronger alignment with the portfolio categorization ME and BE. The association in the value-weighted series is weaker, possibly because value weighting downplays the heterogeneity arising from smaller firms. 

## 7 Conclusion 

This paper studies the modeling of high-dimensional tensor time series under a factor model with multiple change points. Beyond the detection of change points, by formalizing the concept of mode-identifiable changes, we develop a statistic that consistently assigns the detected change  

> 2Retrieved from https://mba.tuck.dartmouth.edu/pages/faculty/ken.french/Data_Library/det_100_por t_sz.html .

28 points to their corresponding modes. This provides an interpretation of structural changes in tensor factor models that is unavailable from detection alone, and underpins the necessity of preserving the multi-dimensional array structure of the data. Moreover, loading matrices for the modes where, either no change occurs or the change is not mode-identifiable, can be estimated by pooling the information across the segments, which leads to a mode-informed estimation strategy with evidently improved estimation accuracy. We construct rigorous theoretical guarantees for our proposed methods, which is further backed up by extensive numerical results. We remark that estimating the number of pseudo-factors, {rk}Kk=1 , might suffer from potentially weak factors or short intervals in practice. To the best of our knowledge, a consistent estimator of factor numbers that can adapt non-linearly spaced changes remains absent in the literature, which certainly merits future investigation. 29 References

A.1 Change point scenarios and their representations 

To demonstrate the generality of our model formulation in (2.1) and (2.2), we showcase some examples of different types of changes, complementing Example 1. For this purpose, it suffices to consider a vector factor model with a single change point, as the conclusions are easily generalized to tensor factor models with multiple change points in different combinations of the modes. Below, all Xt and Et are in Rp1 , where the use of calligraphic letters are simply for consistency with the notations in (2.1) and (2.2). First, rotational changes (i.e., a scale or space change according to Proposition A.1 in Ap-pendix A.2) are straightforwardly encoded in our formulation. That is,

and A2,1, respectively. All other types of changes are characterized with a rank-deficient transformation matrix either before or after the change point. These include, for example, an increase in the number of factors such that

To conclude all the previous discussion, the formulation (2.1) or, equivalently, that in (2.2) is general enough to cover most, if not all (depending on the scope of interests), types of changes in the factor structure. One might argue that changes in the serial correlation in the core factors cannot be formulated, but the serial correlation is not manifested in the static factor representation anyway. This implies the merit of investigating structural changes in dynamic factor models (cf. Cho et al., 2024, for the vector time series case), which we leave for the future study. 

A.2 An anatomy of mode-identifiable changes 

We complement Theorem 1 by categorizing mode-identifiable changes into three types. 

Proposition A.1 (Three types of mode-identifiable change) . Consider any change from Aj,k to 

Aj+1 ,k for j вҲҲ [q] and k вҲҲ [K], and denote by Wj,k вҲҲ Rrk Г—rk the diagonal matrix of singular values of Aj,k in descending order. Let us define the types of changes as follows. (i) Rank change: Aj,k and Aj+1 ,k have different numbers of non-zero singular values. 

36 (ii) Scale change: The change is not a rank change, there does not exist a constant c such that 

Wj+1 ,k = cW j,k , and the space spanned by the non-zero singular values of Aj,k is the same as that of Aj+1 ,k .(iii) Space change: The change is not a rank change nor a scale change, and for any i вҲҲ [rk],the left singular space of Aj,k corresponding to (Wj,k )ii is different from that of Aj+1 ,k 

corresponding to (Wj+1 ,k )ii .Then the change is mode-identifiable if and only if it falls into one of the three types above. 

Proposition A.1 provides a taxonomy of structural changes in tensor factor models, which is comparable with that given by Duan et al. (2025) in the context of vector time series factor modeling. We remark that the distinctions between scale and space changes are ad-hoc, and they only serve to provide more insight on the possible changes in a tensor factor model. It is technically difficult to identify a scale change or a space change, due to the possibility of repeated singular values in the transformation matrix, which cannot be ruled out. On the other hand, estimating the factor numbers (e.g. Lam, 2021; Han et al., 2022; Barigozzi et al., 2026) can lead to the identification of rank changes. 

Proof of Proposition A.1. It is straightforward that when the change falls into any of the three types described in Proposition A.1, it is mode-identifiable according to Theorem 1. It suffices to show if a change is mode-identifiable, then it must fall into one type (note that the types are mutually exclusive by how they are defined), or equivalently, if a change does not fall into any type, then it is mode-unidentifiable. For any j = 1 , . . . , q + 1, we denote the singular value decomposition as

A.3 Relaxation of Assumption 3 (iv) 

Assumption 3 (iv) requires the spacing of the change points to be linear in T . We discuss how this assumption may be relaxed, by presenting an alternative assumption in Appendix A.3.1, and discussing an alteration to TFMseg and its impact on the theoretical results in Appendix A.3.2.

We define some notations used throughout the proof in addition to those given in Introduction. For a random variable X and ОҪ > 0, write вҲҘXвҲҘОҪ = [ E(|X|ОҪ )] 1/ОҪ . Unless specified otherwise, we denote vectors, matrices and tensors by lower-case letters, capital letters, and calligraphic letters, i.e. x, X, and X , respectively. We also use xi, X ij , X iВ·, X В·i to denote, respectively, the i-th element of a vector x, the ( i, j )-th element of X, the i-th row vector (as a column vector) of X, and the 

i-th column vector of X. We use a вүІ b to denote a = O(b), a вүі b to denote b = O(a), and a вүҚ b

to denote a = O(b) and b = O(a); their versions of stochastic order, i.e. with O(В·) replaced by 

OP (В·), are respectively denoted by a вүІP b, b вүіP a, and a вүҚP b. The i-th largest eigenvalue of a matrix A is denoted by О»i(A). We write rank( A) for the rank of a matrix A. For a square matrix A, we write tr( A) for its trace. Also, for a matrix A вҲҲ RmГ—m, vec( A) denotes the vector in Rmn obtained by stacking the columns of A in column-major order, with |A|a = вҲҘvec( A)вҲҘa for 

a вҲҲ { 1, 2, вҲһ} . We use вҠ— to represent the Kronecker product. By convention, the total Kronecker product for an index set is computed in descending index. We write r = QKk=1 rk and r-k = r/r k.

B.1 Proof of Theorem 1 

Lemma B.1. Given any matrices A, B вҲҲ RrГ—r, not necessarily of full rank, such that AA вҠә =

cBB вҠә for some nonzero scalar c, we can express B = cвҲ’1/2AQ for some orthogonal matrix 

Q вҲҲ RrГ—r.Proof of Lemma B.1. Both AA вҠә and BB вҠә are positive semi-definite, and the fact that AA вҠә =

cBB вҠә implies that they have the same eigenvalues (up to scaling by c) and eigenspaces. Let 

A = U ОЈV вҠә be the SVD of A, where U and V are orthogonal matrices and ОЈ is diagonal. Then 

AA вҠә = U ОЈ2U вҠә, and hence BB вҠә = cвҲ’1U ОЈ2U вҠә. The SVD of B is then B = cвҲ’1/2U ОЈW for some orthogonal matrix W , and with AV = U ОЈ, we conclude that B = cвҲ’1/2A(V W ) where V W is orthogonal, as required. 

Lemma B.2. Under the model in (2.2) and Condition 1, Aj,k can only be identified up to an orthogonal transformation for all j вҲҲ [q + 1] and k вҲҲ [K].Proof. Lemmas on functional dependence measures. We define the functional dependence mea-sures of tensor-valued time series, which extend the definitions given by Zhang and Wu (2021) for vector-valued time series. 

Definition B.1 (Functional dependence measures) . Let {Оҫt}tвҲҲZ be a sequence of i.i.d. random elements with zero mean, unit variance, and uniformly bounded fourth moments. Suppose a tensor-valued time series {Y t}tвҲҲZ taking values in Rp1Г—В·В·В·Г— pK , admits the causal representation 

Yt = gt(Оҫt, ОҫtвҲ’1, . . . ), where gt is a (possibly time-varying) measurable map. We further define the coupled process Y(s) 

> t

= gt(Оҫt, . . . , ОҫtвҲ’s+1 , ОҫвҖІ

> tвҲ’s

, ОҫtвҲ’sвҲ’1, . . . ), for s вүҘ 0, where ОҫвҖІ 

> tвҲ’s

is an i.i.d. copy of Оҫ0. For a multi-index i = ( i1, . . . , i K ) вҲҲ [p1] Г— В· В· В· Г— [pK ], let us denote by Yt, i the corresponding entry of Yt. Then we define the element-wise functional dependence coefficients of {Y t}tвҲҲZ as

C.1 Threshold ПҖT,p for change point detection 

We simulate tensor time series under the null model (S0) with no change points in Appendix D.1. Varying the dimensions and core factor tensor ranks as 

T вҲҲ { 400 , 1200 , 2000 , 2800 , 3200 , 4000 , 4800 , 5600 },

(p1, p 2, p 3) вҲҲ { (10 , 10 , 10) , (10 , 10 , 100) , (10 , 20 , 40) , (20 , 20 , 20) },

(r1, r 2, r 3) вҲҲ { (2 , 2, 2) , (2 , 3, 2) , (2 , 3, 3) , (3 , 3, 3) },

while fixing ПҒf = 0 .7, we generate 100 realizations for each combination. Next, for each realization, we take max (s,e ]вҲҲM max s<П„ <e Ts,П„,e , the maximum detector statistic over the collection of seeded intervals, and record the 0 .9-empirical quantiles of these maxima for each setting. Then, we regress them (contained in the R object y) onto the corresponding on functions of T and d =

PKk=1 rk(rk + 1) /2, whose choices are motivated by the approximation of the asymptotic null distribution made in Cho et al. (2026), using the R function lm :

lm(y ~ sqrt(d) + sqrt(log(T)) + log(log(T))/sqrt(log(T)) + 1/sqrt(log(T))) 

The fitted model has R2adj = 0 .9397, and Figure C.1 displays diagnostic plots which show that the model provides a good fit to the data. As the model depends only on T and d, we use it for determining the threshold ПҖT,p .7.5 8.0 8.5 9.0 7.5 8.0 8.5 Fitted values Empirical 0.9-quantiles

n вҲҲ bD.132 D.2.4 (S2) Multi-mode change with equal-spaced change points 

We consider when the change points are equally spaced with Оҳ = вҢҠ0.25 T вҢӢ, вҢҠ0.5T вҢӢ, вҢҠ0.75 T вҢӢ

Comparing the results to those obtained under (S1), we note that the increased size of change as measured by Пүj in Definition 1, thanks to the additional mode undergoing a shift under (S2), benefits TFMseg and leads to its improved performance; this is not the case for LR as vectorization of the data does not preserve the increased change size. For the implementation of mode-wise loading estimation, we focus on the single change sce-nario (S3) in which a rank-deficient transformation matrix A2,2 is applied to the first mode with 

K1 = {1}. Consequently, we have col(Оӣ 1,k ) = col(Оӣ 2,k ) = col(Оӣ k) for k вҲҲ { 2, 3}. Upon consistently detecting and locating Оё1 = вҢҠT / 2вҢӢ by TFMseg and identifying K1 as described in Section 3.2, we compare two approaches to estimating col(Оӣ j,k ), j вҲҲ [2] , k вҲҲ [3]: (M1) A segment-wise estimator that applies the projection-based estimation method described in Section 3.1, to each (0 , bОё1] and ( bОё1, T ], separately. This requires the estimation of rj,k , the mode-k factor number on the j-th segment, which is achieved as described in Section 3.3. (M2) A mode-informed estimator following Remark 3, where Оӣ 1,1 and Оӣ 2,1 are estimated as in (M1) (denoted by bОӣ1,1 and bОӣ2,1, respectively), while Оӣ 2 is estimated as вҲҡp2 times the br2

leading eigenvectors of the matrix 1

bK1 = K1 is returned. Then for the realizations n вҲҲ eD, we measure the distance between the projection matrices onto the corresponding column spaces of the target matrix, say A, and its estimator bA, as 

d( bA, A ) = bA( bAвҠә bA)вҲ’1 bAвҠә вҲ’ A(AвҠәA)вҲ’1AвҠә , (D.1) see Figure D.9. We truncate anomalously large estimation errors from the boxplots for the ease of comparing the results across different settings, noting that the large errors are solely attributed to that the factor number estimator occasionally under-estimates rj,k over shorter segments post change point detection when T is small. 153 As expected, for modes 2 and 3, pooling the pre- and post-change point segments improves the estimation of the loading spaces, and the improvement is observable across all settings. In line with Proposition 1, we note that the mode k loading estimation error decreases with p-k, which is noticeable when the dimensions are highly unbalanced. For mode 1, the post-change estimation error is smaller as rj, 1 = rank(Оӣ j, 1) reduces from 3 to 2 after the change point. This has an adverse effect on the other two modes, when observing the estimation errors form (M1), which is accounted by that the projection involved in (3.1) post-change point is of reduced rank. 154 T = 400 T = 800 T = 1600 T = 3200

Segment post) refers to segment-wise estimation errors using the data before (resp. after) the change point estimator bОё1 by (M1). 155 D.5 Results for change point detection in the presence of missingness 

To investigate the method described in Remark 2 for handling missing observations in the data, we introduce missingness to {X t}Tt=1 generated under (S1) by setting an element Xi1,..., K ,t to be missing if t > вҢҠT / 2вҢӢ and ik > вҢҠpk/2вҢӢ for k вҲҲ [K]. This is a structured missingness pattern explored in Cen and Lam (2025b). Overall, we observe that the proposed modification to TFMseg handles missingness well; the detection of Оё1 is little influenced by the missingness, while detecting Оё2 and Оё3 becomes more challenging as the missingness is concentrated in the second half of the data. It suffers more from the missingness in the presence of serial correlations, which is considered to be more challenging even in the absence of missingness.
